<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Alternative energy</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DanishWindTurbines.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="279" data-file-width="800" height="77" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/DanishWindTurbines.jpg/220px-DanishWindTurbines.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/DanishWindTurbines.jpg/330px-DanishWindTurbines.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/DanishWindTurbines.jpg/440px-DanishWindTurbines.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DanishWindTurbines.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Offshore wind turbines near <a href="/wiki/Copenhagen" title="Copenhagen">Copenhagen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a></div></div></div>
<p><b>Alternative energy</b> is any energy source that is an alternative to <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuel</a>. These alternatives are intended to address concerns about fossil fuels, such as its high <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_emissions" title="Carbon dioxide emissions">carbon dioxide emissions</a>, an important factor in <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a>. <a href="/wiki/Marine_energy" title="Marine energy">Marine energy</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydroelectric_power" title="Hydroelectric power">hydroelectric</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wind_power" title="Wind power">wind</a>, <a href="/wiki/Geothermal_power" title="Geothermal power">geothermal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Solar_power" title="Solar power">solar power</a> are all alternative <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Energy_source" title="Energy source">sources of energy</a>.
</p><p>The nature of what constitutes an alternative energy source has  changed considerably over time, as have controversies regarding energy use.  Because of the variety of energy choices and differing goals of their advocates, defining some energy types as "alternative" is considered very controversial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zehner_2012_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zehner_2012-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definitions">Definitions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Definitions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<table>
<tbody><tr>
<th>Source
</th>
<th>Definition
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td>Oxford Dictionary
</td>
<td>Energy fueled into ways that do not use up natural resources or harm the environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Princeton WordNet
</td>
<td>Energy derived from sources that do not use up natural resources or harm the environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Responding to Climate Change 2007
</td>
<td>Energy derived from nontraditional sources (e.g., compressed natural gas, solar, hydroelectric, wind).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Natural Resources Defense Council
</td>
<td>Energy that is not popularly used and is usually environmentally sound, such as solar or wind energy (as opposed to fossil fuels).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Materials Management
</td>
<td>Fuel sources that are other than those derived from fossil fuels. Typically used interchangeably for <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">renewable energy</a>. Examples include: wind, solar, biomass, wave and tidal energy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Torridge" title="Torridge">Torridge</a> District Council
</td>
<td>Energy generated from alternatives to fossil fuels. Need not be renewable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Historians of economies have examined the key transitions to alternative energies and regard the transitions as pivotal in bringing about significant economic change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>  Prior to the shift to an alternative energy, supplies of the dominant energy type became erratic, accompanied by rapid increases in energy prices.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coal_as_an_alternative_to_wood">Coal as an alternative to wood</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Coal as an alternative to wood">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the late <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medieval" title="Medieval">medieval</a> period, <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">coal</a> was the new alternative fuel to save the society from overuse of the dominant fuel, wood. The <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a> had resulted in shortage of wood, at that time soft coal appeared as a savior. Historian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Norman_F._Cantor" title="Norman F. Cantor">Norman F. Cantor</a> describes how:
</p>
<blockquote><p>Europeans had lived in the midst of vast forests throughout the earlier medieval centuries. After 1250 they became so skilled at deforestation that by 1500 AD they were running short of wood for heating and cooking... By 1500 Europe was on the edge of a fuel and nutritional disaster, [from] which it was saved in the sixteenth century only by the burning of soft coal and the cultivation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Potatoes" title="Potatoes">potatoes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">maize</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Petroleum_as_an_alternative_to_whale_oil">Petroleum as an alternative to whale oil</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Petroleum as an alternative to whale oil">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Whale_oil" title="Whale oil">Whale oil</a> was the dominant form of lubrication and fuel for lamps in the early 19th century, but the <a href="/wiki/History_of_whaling" title="History of whaling">depletion of the whale stocks</a> by mid century caused whale oil prices to skyrocket setting the stage for the adoption of petroleum which was first commercialized in <a href="/wiki/Titusville,_Pennsylvania" title="Titusville, Pennsylvania">Pennsylvania</a> in 1859.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethanol_as_an_alternative_to_fossil_fuels">Ethanol as an alternative to fossil fuels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Ethanol as an alternative to fossil fuels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_fuel" title="Ethanol fuel">Ethanol fuel</a></div>
<p>In 1917, <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Graham_Bell" title="Alexander Graham Bell">Alexander Graham Bell</a> advocated ethanol from corn, wheat and other foods as an alternative to coal and oil, stating that the world was in measurable distance of depleting these fuels.  For Bell, the problem requiring an alternative was lack of renewability of orthodox energy sources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> Since the 1970s, <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_fuel_in_Brazil" title="Ethanol fuel in Brazil">Brazil has had an ethanol fuel program</a> which has allowed the country to become the world's second largest producer of <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">ethanol</a> (after the United States) and the world's largest exporter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RFA1E_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-RFA1E-14">[14]</a></sup> Brazil's ethanol fuel program uses modern equipment and cheap <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sugar_cane" title="Sugar cane">sugar cane</a> as feedstock, and the residual cane-waste (<a href="/wiki/Bagasse" title="Bagasse">bagasse</a>) is used to process heat and power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MLA_2004_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-MLA_2004-15">[15]</a></sup> There are no longer light vehicles in Brazil running on pure gasoline. By the end of 2008 there were 35,000 filling stations throughout Brazil with at least one ethanol pump.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wilson_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wilson-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Cellulosic_ethanol" title="Cellulosic ethanol">Cellulosic ethanol</a> can be produced from a diverse array of feedstocks, and involves the use of the whole crop.  This new approach should increase yields and reduce the <a href="/wiki/Carbon_footprint" title="Carbon footprint">carbon footprint</a> because the amount of energy-intensive fertilizers and fungicides will remain the same, for a higher output of usable material.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cellu_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-cellu-18">[18]</a></sup> As of 2008, there are nine <a href="/wiki/Cellulosic_ethanol_commercialization" title="Cellulosic ethanol commercialization">commercial cellulosic ethanol plants</a> which are either operating, or under construction, in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Second-generation_biofuels" title="Second-generation biofuels">Second-generation biofuels</a> technologies are able to manufacture biofuels from inedible biomass and could hence prevent conversion of food into fuel."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> As of July 2010, there is one commercial second-generation (2G) ethanol plant <a href="/wiki/Inbicon" title="Inbicon">Inbicon Biomass Refinery</a>, which is operating in Denmark.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coal_gasification_as_an_alternative_to_petroleum">Coal gasification as an alternative to petroleum</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Coal gasification as an alternative to petroleum">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the 1970s, President <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Carter" title="Jimmy Carter">Jimmy Carter</a>'s administration advocated <a href="/wiki/Coal_gasification" title="Coal gasification">coal gasification</a> as an alternative to expensive imported oil.  The program, including the <a href="/wiki/Synthetic_Fuels_Corporation" title="Synthetic Fuels Corporation">Synthetic Fuels Corporation</a> was scrapped when petroleum prices plummeted in the 1980s. The carbon footprint and <a href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_of_the_coal_industry" title="Environmental impact of the coal industry">environmental impact of coal</a> gasification are both very high.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Existing_types_of_alternative_energy">Existing types of alternative energy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Existing types of alternative energy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydro_electricity" title="Hydro electricity">Hydro electricity</a> captures energy from falling water.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power" title="Nuclear power">Nuclear energy</a> uses nuclear fission to release energy stored in the atomic bonds of heavy elements.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wind_energy" title="Wind energy">Wind energy</a> is the generation of electricity from wind, commonly by using propeller-like turbines.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Solar_energy" title="Solar energy">Solar energy</a> is the use of energy from the sun. Heat from the sun can be used for <a href="/wiki/Solar_thermal_energy" title="Solar thermal energy">solar thermal applications</a> or light can be converted into electricity via photovoltaic devices.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Geothermal_energy" title="Geothermal energy">Geothermal energy</a> is the use of the earth's internal heat to boil water for heating buildings or generating electricity.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Biofuel" title="Biofuel">Biofuel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">ethanol</a> are plant-derived gasoline substitutes for powering vehicles.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_economy" title="Hydrogen economy">Hydrogen</a> can be used as a carrier of energy, produced by various technologies such as cracking of hydrocarbons or water electrolysis.</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Enabling_technologies">Enabling technologies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Enabling technologies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ice_storage_air_conditioning" title="Ice storage air conditioning">Ice storage air conditioning</a> and <a href="/wiki/Storage_heater" title="Storage heater">thermal storage heaters</a> are methods of shifting consumption to use low cost off-peak electricity.  When compared to resistance heating, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heat_pumps" title="Heat pumps">heat pumps</a>  conserve electrical power (or in rare cases mechanical or thermal power) by collecting heat from a cool source such as a body of water, the ground or the air.
</p><p>Thermal storage technologies allow heat or cold to be stored for periods of time ranging from diurnal to <a href="/wiki/Seasonal_thermal_energy_storage" title="Seasonal thermal energy storage">interseasonal</a>, and can involve storage of <a href="/wiki/Sensible_heat" title="Sensible heat">sensible energy</a> (i.e. by changing the temperature of a medium) or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latent_energy" title="Latent energy">latent energy</a> (e.g. through phase changes of a medium (i.e. changes from solid to liquid or vice versa), such as between water and slush or ice). Energy sources can be natural (via solar-thermal collectors, or dry cooling towers used to collect winter's cold), waste energy (such as from HVAC equipment, industrial processes or power plants), or surplus energy (such as seasonally from hydropower projects or intermittently from wind farms). The <a href="/wiki/Drake_Landing_Solar_Community" title="Drake Landing Solar Community">Drake Landing Solar Community</a> (Alberta, Canada) is illustrative. <a href="/wiki/Seasonal_thermal_energy_storage" title="Seasonal thermal energy storage">Borehole thermal energy storage</a> allows the community to get 97% of its year-round heat from solar collectors on the garage roofs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> The storages can be insulated tanks, borehole clusters in substrates ranging from gravel to bedrock, deep aquifers, or shallow pits that are lined and insulated.  Some applications require inclusion of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heat_pumps" title="Heat pumps">heat pump</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Renewable_energy_vs_non-renewable_energy">Renewable energy vs non-renewable energy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Renewable energy vs non-renewable energy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">Renewable energy</a></div>
<p>Renewable energy is generated from <a href="/wiki/Natural_resource" title="Natural resource">natural resources</a>—such as <a href="/wiki/Sunlight" title="Sunlight">sunlight</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Wind" title="Wind">wind</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rain" title="Rain">rain</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tidal_energy" title="Tidal energy">tides</a> and <a href="/wiki/Geothermal_energy" title="Geothermal energy">geothermal heat</a>—which are renewable (naturally replenished).  When comparing the processes for producing energy, there remain several fundamental differences between renewable energy and fossil fuels.  The process of producing oil, coal, or natural gas fuel is a difficult and demanding process that requires a great deal of complex equipment, physical and chemical processes. On the other hand, alternative energy can be widely produced with basic equipment and natural processes.  Wood, the most renewable and available alternative fuel, emits the same amount of carbon when burned as would be emitted if it degraded naturally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Nuclear power is an alternative to fossil fuels that is non-renewable, like fossil fuels, nuclear ones are a finite resource.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ecologically_friendly_alternatives">Ecologically friendly alternatives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Ecologically friendly alternatives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A renewable energy source such as <a href="/wiki/Biomass" title="Biomass">biomass</a> is sometimes regarded as a good alternative to providing heat and electricity with fossil fuels. Biofuels are not inherently ecologically friendly for this purpose, while burning biomass is carbon-neutral, air pollution is still produced. For example, the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>, once leader in use of <a href="/wiki/Palm_oil" title="Palm oil">palm oil</a> as a biofuel, has suspended all subsidies for palm oil due to the scientific evidence that their use "may sometimes create more environmental harm than fossil fuels".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pal_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-pal-26">[26]</a></sup> The Netherlands government and environmental groups are trying to trace the origins of imported palm oil, to certify which operations produce the oil in a responsible manner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pal_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-pal-26">[26]</a></sup> Regarding biofuels from foodstuffs, the realization that converting the entire grain harvest of the US would only produce 16% of its auto fuel needs, and the decimation of Brazil's CO<sub>2</sub> absorbing tropical rain forests to make way for biofuel production has made it clear that placing energy markets in competition with food markets results in higher food prices and insignificant or negative impact on energy issues such as global warming or dependence on foreign energy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>  Recently, alternatives to such undesirable sustainable fuels are being sought, such as commercially viable sources of cellulosic ethanol.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Relatively_new_concepts_for_alternative_energy">Relatively new concepts for alternative energy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Relatively new concepts for alternative energy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Carbon-neutral_and_negative_fuels">Carbon-neutral and negative fuels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Carbon-neutral and negative fuels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Carbon-neutral_fuel" title="Carbon-neutral fuel">Carbon-neutral fuel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Methanol_economy" title="Methanol economy">Methanol economy</a></div>
<p>Carbon-neutral fuels are <a href="/wiki/Synthetic_fuel" title="Synthetic fuel">synthetic fuels</a> (including <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gasoline" title="Gasoline">gasoline</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diesel_fuel" title="Diesel fuel">diesel fuel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jet_fuel" title="Jet fuel">jet fuel</a> or <a href="/wiki/Ammonia#As_a_fuel" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>) produced by <a href="/wiki/Hydrogenation" title="Hydrogenation">hydrogenating</a> waste <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> recycled from <a href="/wiki/Flue-gas_emissions_from_fossil-fuel_combustion" title="Flue-gas emissions from fossil-fuel combustion">power plant flue-gas emissions</a>, recovered from automotive <a href="/wiki/Exhaust_gas" title="Exhaust gas">exhaust gas</a>, or derived from <a href="/wiki/Carbonic_acid" title="Carbonic acid">carbonic acid</a> in <a href="/wiki/Seawater" title="Seawater">seawater</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Graves2011rev_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Graves2011rev-29">[29]</a></sup> Commercial fuel synthesis companies suggest they can produce synthetic fuels for less than <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">petroleum</a> fuels when oil costs more than $55 per barrel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> Renewable methanol (RM) is a fuel produced from <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> and carbon dioxide by catalytic <a href="/wiki/Hydrogenation" title="Hydrogenation">hydrogenation</a> where the hydrogen has been obtained from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Water_electrolysis" title="Water electrolysis">water electrolysis</a>. It can be blended into transportation fuel or processed as a chemical feedstock.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/George_Olah" title="George Olah">George Olah</a> carbon dioxide recycling plant operated by <a href="/wiki/Carbon_Recycling_International" title="Carbon Recycling International">Carbon Recycling International</a> in <a href="/wiki/Grindav%C3%ADk" title="Grindavík">Grindavík</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a> has been producing 2 million liters of <a href="/wiki/Methanol" title="Methanol">methanol</a> transportation fuel per year from flue exhaust of the <a href="/wiki/Svartsengi_Power_Station" title="Svartsengi Power Station">Svartsengi Power Station</a> since 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> It has the capacity to produce 5 million liters per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> A 250 kilowatt methane synthesis plant was constructed by the Center for Solar Energy and Hydrogen Research (ZSW) at <a href="/wiki/Baden-W%C3%BCrttemberg" title="Baden-Württemberg">Baden-Württemberg</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Fraunhofer_Society" title="Fraunhofer Society">Fraunhofer Society</a> in Germany and began operating in 2010. It is being upgraded to 10 megawatts, scheduled for completion in autumn, 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zsw-bw1_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-zsw-bw1-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zsw-bw2_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-zsw-bw2-35">[35]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Audi" title="Audi">Audi</a> has constructed a carbon-neutral <a href="/wiki/Liquefied_natural_gas" title="Liquefied natural gas">liquefied natural gas</a> (LNG) plant in <a href="/wiki/Werlte" title="Werlte">Werlte</a>, Germany.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> The plant is intended to produce transportation fuel to offset LNG used in their <a href="/wiki/Audi_A3#A3_Sportback_g-tron_(2013-)" title="Audi A3">A3 Sportback g-tron</a> automobiles, and can keep 2,800 metric tons of CO<sub>2</sub> out of the environment per year at its initial capacity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> Other commercial developments are taking place in <a href="/wiki/Columbia,_South_Carolina" title="Columbia, South Carolina">Columbia, South Carolina</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Camarillo,_California" title="Camarillo, California">Camarillo, California</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Darlington" title="Darlington">Darlington</a>, England.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="vertical-navbox nowraplinks hlist" style="float:right;clear:right;width:22.0em;margin:0 0 1.0em 1.0em;background:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;padding:0.2em;border-spacing:0.4em 0;text-align:center;line-height:1.4em;font-size:88%"><tbody><tr><td style="padding-top:0.4em;line-height:1.2em">Part of <a href="/wiki/Category:Environmental_economics" title="Category:Environmental economics">a series</a> about</td></tr><tr><th style="padding:0.2em 0.4em 0.2em;padding-top:0;font-size:145%;line-height:1.2em"><a href="/wiki/Environmental_economics" title="Environmental economics">Environmental economics</a></th></tr><tr><th style="padding:0.1em">

</tr><tr><th style="padding:0.1em">
Carbon related</th></tr><tr><td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Low-carbon_economy" title="Low-carbon economy">Low-carbon economy</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_neutral_fuel" title="Carbon neutral fuel">Carbon neutral fuel</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Carbon_neutrality" title="Carbon neutrality">Carbon neutrality</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_pricing" title="Carbon pricing">Carbon pricing</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Emissions_trading" title="Emissions trading">Emissions trading</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Carbon_credit" title="Carbon credit">Carbon credit</a></li>
