<li><a href="/wiki/Carbon_tax" title="Carbon tax">Carbon tax</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Carbon_finance" title="Carbon finance">Carbon finance</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Feed-in_tariff" title="Feed-in tariff">Feed-in tariff</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Carbon_diet" title="Carbon diet">Carbon diet</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Food_miles" title="Food miles">Food miles</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/2000-watt_society" title="2000-watt society">2000-watt society</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Carbon_footprint" title="Carbon footprint">Carbon footprint</a></li></ul></td>
</tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Green_economics_sidebar" title="Template:Green economics sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Green_economics_sidebar" title="Template talk:Green economics sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Green_economics_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Such fuels are considered <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon-neutral" title="Carbon-neutral">carbon-neutral</a> because they do not result in a net increase in atmospheric <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gases</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lackner2012_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lackner2012-41">[41]</a></sup> To the extent that synthetic fuels displace <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuels</a>, or if they are produced from waste carbon or seawater carbonic acid,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eisaman2012_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Eisaman2012-42">[42]</a></sup> and their combustion is subject to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_capture" title="Carbon capture">carbon capture</a> at the flue or exhaust pipe, they result in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Negative_carbon_dioxide_emission" title="Negative carbon dioxide emission">negative carbon dioxide emission</a> and net <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_removal" title="Carbon dioxide removal">carbon dioxide removal</a> from the atmosphere, and thus constitute a form of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas_remediation" title="Greenhouse gas remediation">greenhouse gas remediation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goeppert2012_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goeppert2012-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>Such renewable fuels alleviate the costs and dependency issues of imported fossil fuels without requiring either electrification of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vehicle_fleet" title="Vehicle fleet">vehicle fleet</a> or conversion to hydrogen or other fuels, enabling continued compatible and affordable vehicles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pearson2012_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pearson2012-44">[44]</a></sup> Carbon-neutral fuels offer relatively low cost energy <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Power_storage#Synthetic_hydrocarbon_fuel" title="Power storage">storage</a>, alleviating the problems of wind and solar <a href="/wiki/Intermittency" title="Intermittency">intermittency</a>, and they enable distribution of wind, water, and solar power through existing natural gas pipelines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pearson2012_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pearson2012-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>Nighttime <a href="/wiki/Wind_power" title="Wind power">wind power</a> is considered the most economical form of electrical power with which to synthesize fuel, because the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Load_curve" title="Load curve">load curve</a> for electricity peaks sharply during the day, but wind tends to blow slightly more at night than during the day, so, the price of nighttime wind power is often much less expensive than any alternative.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pearson2012_44-2"><a href="#cite_note-Pearson2012-44">[44]</a></sup> Germany has built a 250 kilowatt synthetic methane plant which they are scaling up to 10 megawatts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zsw-bw1_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-zsw-bw1-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zsw-bw2_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-zsw-bw2-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Algae_fuel">Algae fuel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Algae fuel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Algae_fuel" title="Algae fuel">Algae fuel</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Biofuel" title="Biofuel">biofuel</a> which is derived from algae. During photosynthesis, algae and other photosynthetic organisms capture carbon dioxide and sunlight and convert it into oxygen and biomass. This is usually done by placing the algae between two panes of glass.  The algae creates three forms of energy fuel: heat (from its growth cycle), biofuel (the natural "oil" derived from the algae), and biomass (from the algae itself, as it is harvested upon maturity).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>The heat can be used to power building systems (such as heat process water) or to produce energy.  Biofuel is oil extracted from the algae upon maturity, and used to create energy similar to the use of <a href="/wiki/Biodiesel" title="Biodiesel">biodiesel</a>. The biomass is the matter left over after extracting the oil and water, and can be harvested to produce combustible methane for energy production, similar to the warmth felt in a compost pile or the methane collected from biodegradable materials in a landfill.  Additionally, the benefits of algae biofuel are that it can be produced industrially, as well as vertically (i.e. as a building facade), thereby obviating the use of arable land and food crops (such as soy, palm, and canola).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biomass_briquettes">Biomass briquettes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Biomass briquettes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Biomass_briquettes" title="Biomass briquettes">Biomass briquettes</a> are being developed in the developing world as an alternative to charcoal.  The technique involves the conversion of almost any plant matter into compressed briquettes that typically have about 70% the calorific value of charcoal.  There are relatively few examples of large scale briquette production. One exception is in North Kivu, in eastern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_Congo" title="Democratic Republic of Congo">Democratic Republic of Congo</a>, where forest clearance for charcoal production is considered to be the biggest threat to Mountain Gorilla habitat.  The staff of <a href="/wiki/Virunga_National_Park" title="Virunga National Park">Virunga National Park</a> have successfully trained and equipped over 3500 people to produce biomass briquettes, thereby replacing charcoal produced illegally inside the national park, and creating significant employment for people living in extreme poverty in conflict affected areas.
<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Biogas_digestion">Biogas digestion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Biogas digestion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Biogas" title="Biogas">Biogas</a> digestion harnesses the methane gas that is released when organic waste breaks down in an anaerobic environment. This gas can be retrieved from landfill sites or sewage systems. The gas can be used as a fuel for heat  or, more commonly, electricity generation.
<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
The methane gas that is collected and refined can be used as an energy source for various products.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biological_hydrogen_production">Biological hydrogen production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Biological hydrogen production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Hydrogen gas is a completely clean burning fuel; its only by-product is water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> It also contains relatively high amount of energy compared with other fuels due to its chemical structure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>2H<sub>2</sub> + O<sub>2</sub> → 2H<sub>2</sub>O + High Energy
</p><p>High Energy + 2H<sub>2</sub>O →  2H<sub>2</sub> + O<sub>2</sub>
</p><p>This requires a high-energy input, making commercial hydrogen very inefficient.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>  Use of a biological vector as a means to split water, and therefore produce hydrogen gas, would allow for the only energy input to be solar radiation. Biological vectors can include bacteria or more commonly algae. This process is known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biological_hydrogen_production_(Algae)" title="Biological hydrogen production (Algae)">biological hydrogen production</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup>  It requires the use of single celled organisms to create hydrogen gas through fermentation. Without the presence of oxygen, also known as an anaerobic environment, regular cellular respiration cannot take place and a process known as fermentation takes over. A major by-product of this process is hydrogen gas. If this could be implemented on a large scale, then sunlight, nutrients and water could create hydrogen gas to be used as a dense source of energy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> Large-scale production has proven difficult. Not until 1999, was it even possible to induce these anaerobic conditions by sulfur deprivation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> Since the fermentation process is an evolutionary back up, turned on during stress, the cells would die after a few days. In 2000, a two-stage process was developed to take the cells in and out of anaerobic conditions and therefore keep them alive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>  For the last ten years, finding a way to do this on a large-scale has been the main goal of research. Careful work is being done to ensure an efficient process before large-scale production, however once a mechanism is developed, this type of production could solve our energy needs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hydroelectricity">Hydroelectricity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Hydroelectricity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hydroelectricity" title="Hydroelectricity">Hydroelectricity</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Hydroelectricity" title="Hydroelectricity">Hydroelectricity</a> provided 75% of the worlds renewable electricity in 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> Much of the electricity used today is a result of the heyday of conventional hydroelectric development between 1960 and 1980, which has virtually ceased in Europe and North America due to environmental concerns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> Globally there is a trend towards more hydroelectricity. From 2004 to 2014 the installed capacity rose from 715 to 1,055 GW.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> A popular alternative to the large dams of the past is <a href="/wiki/Run-of-the-river_hydroelectricity" title="Run-of-the-river hydroelectricity">run-of-the-river</a> where there is no water stored behind a dam and generation usually varies with seasonal rainfall. Using run-of-the-river in wet seasons and solar in dry seasons can balance seasonal variations for both. Another move away from large dams is <a href="/wiki/Small_hydro" title="Small hydro">small hydro</a>, these tend to be situated high up on tributaries, rather than on main rivers in valley bottoms.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Offshore_wind">Offshore wind</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Offshore wind">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Offshore <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wind_farms" title="Wind farms">wind farms</a> are similar to land-based wind farms, but are located on the ocean. Offshore wind farms can be placed in water up to 40 metres (130 ft) deep, whereas floating wind turbines can float in water up to 700 metres (2,300 ft) deep.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-works_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-works-60">[60]</a></sup>
The advantage of having a floating wind farm is to be able to harness the winds from the open ocean. Without any obstructions such as hills, trees and buildings, winds from the open ocean can reach up to speeds twice as fast as coastal areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-works_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-works-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>Significant generation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Offshore_wind_energy" title="Offshore wind energy">offshore wind energy</a> already contributes to electricity needs in <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a> and now the first offshore wind farms are under development in U.S. waters. While the offshore wind industry has grown dramatically over the last several decades, especially in Europe, there is still uncertainty associated with how the construction and operation of these wind farms affect marine animals and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marine_environment" title="Marine environment">marine environment</a>.
</p><p>Traditional offshore <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wind_turbines" title="Wind turbines">wind turbines</a> are attached to the seabed in shallower waters within the nearshore marine environment. As offshore wind technologies become more advanced, floating structures have begun to be used in deeper waters where more wind resources exist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tethys_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tethys-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Marine_and_hydrokinetic_energy">Marine and hydrokinetic energy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Marine and hydrokinetic energy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Marine and Hydrokinetic (MHK) or <a href="/wiki/Marine_energy" title="Marine energy">marine energy</a> development includes projects using the following devices:
</p>
<ul><li>Wave power is the transport of energy by <a href="/wiki/Wind_wave" title="Wind wave">wind waves</a>, and the capture of that energy to do useful work – for example, <a href="/wiki/Electricity_generation" title="Electricity generation">electricity generation</a> or <a href="/wiki/Pump" title="Pump">pumping</a> water into reservoirs. A machine able to exploit significant waves in open coastal areas is generally known as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wave_energy" title="Wave energy">wave energy converter</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Tidal_power" title="Tidal power">Tidal power</a> turbines are placed in coastal and estuarine areas and daily flows are quite predictable.</li>
<li>In-stream turbines in fast-moving rivers</li>
<li>Ocean current turbines in areas of strong marine currents</li>
<li>Ocean thermal energy converters in deep tropical waters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tethys_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tethys-62">[62]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nuclear_power">Nuclear power</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Nuclear power">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the year 2015 ten new reactors came online and 67 more were under construction including the first eight new  Generation III+ <a href="/wiki/AP1000" title="AP1000">AP1000</a> reactors in the US and China and the first four new Generation III <a href="/wiki/EPR_(nuclear_reactor)" title="EPR (nuclear reactor)">EPR</a> reactors in Finland, France and China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> Reactors are also under construction in Belarus, Brazil, India, Iran, Japan, Pakistan, Russia, Slovakia, South Korea, Turkey, Ukraine and United Arab Emirates.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Thorium_nuclear_power">Thorium nuclear power</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Thorium nuclear power">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Thorium_reactor_ORNL.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="446" data-file-width="601" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Thorium_reactor_ORNL.jpg/200px-Thorium_reactor_ORNL.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Thorium_reactor_ORNL.jpg/300px-Thorium_reactor_ORNL.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Thorium_reactor_ORNL.jpg/400px-Thorium_reactor_ORNL.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Thorium_reactor_ORNL.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Early thorium-based (<a href="/wiki/Molten_salt_reactor" title="Molten salt reactor">MSR</a>) nuclear reactor at <a href="/wiki/Oak_Ridge_National_Laboratory" title="Oak Ridge National Laboratory">Oak Ridge National Laboratory</a> in the 1960s</div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Thorium_fuel_cycle" title="Thorium fuel cycle">Thorium fuel cycle</a></div><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information on power production see: <a href="/wiki/Thorium-based_nuclear_power" title="Thorium-based nuclear power">Thorium-based nuclear power</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Thorium" title="Thorium">Thorium</a> is a fissionable material for possible future use in a <a href="/wiki/Thorium-based_nuclear_power" title="Thorium-based nuclear power">thorium-based reactor</a>. Proponents of thorium reactors claims several potential advantages over a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Uranium_fuel_cycle" title="Uranium fuel cycle">uranium fuel cycle</a>, such as thorium's <a href="/wiki/Thorium#Occurrence" title="Thorium">greater abundance</a>, better resistance to <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_proliferation" title="Nuclear proliferation">nuclear weapons proliferation</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hargraves-Moir_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hargraves-Moir-66">[66]</a></sup> and reduced <a href="/wiki/Plutonium" title="Plutonium">plutonium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Actinide" title="Actinide">actinide</a> production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hargraves-Moir_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hargraves-Moir-66">[66]</a></sup>  Thorium reactors can be modified to produce <a href="/wiki/Uranium-233" title="Uranium-233">Uranium-233</a>, which can then be processed into highly enriched uranium, which has been tested in low yield weapons,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> and is unproven on a commercial scale.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Investing_in_alternative_energy">Investing in alternative energy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Investing in alternative energy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>As an emerging economic sector, there are limited stock market investment opportunities in alternative energy available to the general public. The public can buy shares of alternative energy companies from various stock markets, with wildly volatile returns. The recent IPO of SolarCity demonstrates the nascent nature of this sector- within a few weeks, it already had achieved the second highest market cap within the alternative energy sector.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p>Investors can also choose to invest in ETFs (exchange-traded funds) that track an alternative energy index, such as the WilderHill New Energy Index.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> Additionally, there are a number of mutual funds, such as Calvert's Global Alternative Energy Mutual Fund<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup>   that are a bit more proactive in choosing the selected investments.
</p><p>Recently, <a href="/wiki/Mosaic_Inc." title="Mosaic Inc.">Mosaic Inc.</a> launched an online platform allowing residents of California and New York to invest directly in solar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> Investing in solar projects had previously been limited to accredited investors,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> or a small number of willing banks.
</p><p>Over the last three years publicly traded alternative energy companies have been very volatile, with some 2007 returns in excess of 100%, some 2008 returns down 90% or more, and peak-to-trough returns in 2009 again over 100%.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>  In general there are three sub-segments of "alternative" energy investment:  solar energy, wind energy and hybrid electric vehicles. Alternative energy sources which are renewable and have lower carbon emissions than fossil fuels are hydropower, wind energy, solar energy, geothermal energy, and bio fuels. Each of these four segments involve very different technologies and investment concerns.
</p><p>For example, photovoltaic solar energy is based on semiconductor processing and accordingly, benefits from steep cost reductions similar to those realized in the microprocessor industry (i.e., driven by larger scale, higher module efficiency, and improving processing technologies). PV solar energy is perhaps the only energy technology whose electricity generation cost could be reduced by half or more over the next five years. Better and more efficient manufacturing process and new technology such as advanced thin film solar cell is a good example of that helps to reduce industry cost.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p>The economics of solar PV electricity are highly dependent on silicon pricing and even companies whose technologies are based on other materials (e.g., First Solar) are impacted by the balance of supply and demand in the silicon market.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>  In addition, because some companies sell completed solar cells on the open market (e.g., Q-Cells), this creates a low barrier to entry for companies that want to manufacture solar modules, which in turn can create an irrational pricing environment.
</p><p>In contrast, because wind power has been harnessed for over 100 years, its underlying technology is relatively stable.  Its economics are largely determined by siting (e.g., how hard the wind blows and the grid investment requirements) and the prices of steel (the largest component of a wind turbine) and select composites (used for the blades).  Because current wind turbines are often in excess of 100 meters high, logistics and a global manufacturing platform are major sources of competitive advantage.  These issues and others were explored in a research report by Sanford Bernstein.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alternative_energy_in_transportation">Alternative energy in transportation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Alternative energy in transportation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Due to steadily rising gas prices in 2008 with the US national average price per gallon of regular unleaded gas rising above $4.00 at one point,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> there has been a steady movement towards developing higher fuel efficiency and more <a href="/wiki/Alternative_fuel_vehicle" title="Alternative fuel vehicle">alternative fuel vehicles</a> for consumers. In response, many smaller companies have rapidly increased research and development into radically different ways of powering consumer vehicles. <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_electric_vehicle" title="Hybrid electric vehicle">Hybrid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Battery_electric_vehicle" title="Battery electric vehicle">battery electric vehicles</a> are commercially available and are gaining wider industry and consumer acceptance worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p><p>For example, Nissan USA introduced the world's first mass-production electric vehicle, the <a href="/wiki/Nissan_Leaf" title="Nissan Leaf">Nissan Leaf</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> A plug-in hybrid car, the <a href="/wiki/Chevrolet_Volt" title="Chevrolet Volt">Chevrolet Volt</a> also has been produced, using an electric motor to drive the wheels, and a small four-cylinder engine to generate additional electricity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Making_alternative_energy_mainstream">Making alternative energy mainstream</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Making alternative energy mainstream">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Before alternative energy becomes mainstream there are a few crucial obstacles that it must overcome. First there must be increased understanding of how alternative energies are beneficial; secondly the availability components for these systems must increase; and lastly the pay-back period must be decreased.
</p><p>For example, <a href="/wiki/Electric_vehicle" title="Electric vehicle">electric vehicles</a> (EV) and <a href="/wiki/Plug-in_hybrid" title="Plug-in hybrid">plug-in hybrid electric vehicles</a> (PHEV) are on the rise. The continue adoption of these vehicles depend on investment in public charging infrastructure, as well as implementing much more alternative energy for future transportation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Research">Research</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Research">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>There are numerous organizations within the academic, federal, and commercial sectors conducting large scale advanced research in the field of alternative energy. This research spans several areas of focus across the alternative energy spectrum. Most of the research is targeted at improving efficiency and increasing overall energy yields.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the US, multiple federally supported research organizations have focused on alternative energy in recent years. Two of the most prominent of these labs are <a href="/wiki/Sandia_National_Laboratories" title="Sandia National Laboratories">Sandia National Laboratories</a> and the <a href="/wiki/National_Renewable_Energy_Laboratory" title="National Renewable Energy Laboratory">National Renewable Energy Laboratory</a> (NREL), both of which are funded by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Energy" title="United States Department of Energy">United States Department of Energy</a> and supported by various corporate partners.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> Sandia has a total budget of $2.4 billion<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> while NREL has a budget of $375 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>With the increasing consumption levels of energy, it is projected that the levels would increase by 21% in 2030.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> The cost of the renewables was relatively cheaper at $2.5m/MW as compared to the non-renewables &amp; 2.7m/MW.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> Evidently, the use of renewable energy is a cost effective method of obtaining energy. Additionally, their use also dispenses with the trade-off that has existed between environmental conservation and economic growth.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mechanical_energy">Mechanical energy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Mechanical energy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Mechanical energy associated with human activities such as blood circulation, respiration, walking, typing and running, is ubiquitous but usually wasted. It has attracted tremendous attention from researchers around the globe to find methods to scavenge such mechanical energies. The best solution currently is to use piezoelectric materials, which can generate flow of electrons when deformed. Various devices using piezoelectric materials have been built to scavenge mechanical energy. Considering that the piezoelectric constant of the material plays a critical role in the overall performance of a piezoelectric device, one critical research direction to improve device efficiency is to find new material of large piezoelectric response. Lead Magnesium Niobate-Lead Titanate (PMN-PT) is a next-generation piezoelectric material with super high piezoelectric constant when ideal composition and orientation are obtained. In 2012, PMN-PT Nanowires with a very high piezoelectric constant were fabricated by a hydro-thermal approach<sup><a href="/wiki/Nanogenerator" title="Nanogenerator">[14]</a></sup> and then assembled into an energy-harvesting device<sup><a href="/wiki/Nanogenerator" title="Nanogenerator">[15]</a></sup>. The record-high piezoelectric constant was further improved by the fabrication of a single-crystal PMN-PT nanobelt,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> which was then used as the essential building block for a piezoelectric nanogenerator.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Solar">Solar</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Solar">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Solar_energy" title="Solar energy">Solar energy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Solar_One_Power_Plant_1993_California.jpg"><img alt="Solar One Power Plant 1993 California.jpg" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="397" data-file-width="500" height="175" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Solar_One_Power_Plant_1993_California.jpg/220px-Solar_One_Power_Plant_1993_California.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Solar_One_Power_Plant_1993_California.jpg/330px-Solar_One_Power_Plant_1993_California.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Solar_One_Power_Plant_1993_California.jpg/440px-Solar_One_Power_Plant_1993_California.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Solar_One_Power_Plant_1993_California.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Solar_energy" title="Solar energy">Solar energy</a> can be used for <a href="/wiki/Solar_thermal_energy" title="Solar thermal energy">heating</a>, <a href="/wiki/Solar_air_conditioning" title="Solar air conditioning">cooling</a> or electrical power generation using the sun.
</p><p>Solar heat has long been employed in <a href="/wiki/Passive_solar_building_design" title="Passive solar building design">passively</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Active_solar_heating" title="Active solar heating">actively</a> heated buildings, as well as district heating systems. Examples of the latter are the <a href="/wiki/Drake_Landing_Solar_Community" title="Drake Landing Solar Community">Drake Landing Solar Community</a> is Alberta, Canada, and numerous district systems in Denmark<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> and Germany.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> In Europe, there are two programs for the application of solar heat: the Solar District Heating (SDH)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/International_Energy_Agency" title="International Energy Agency">International Energy Agency</a>'s Solar Heating and Cooling (SHC) program.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup>
</p><p>The obstacles preventing the large-scale implementation of solar powered energy generation is the inefficiency of current solar technology and the cost. Currently, photovoltaic (PV) panels only have the ability to convert around 16% of the sunlight that hits them into electricity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup>
</p><p>Both <a href="/wiki/Sandia_National_Laboratories" title="Sandia National Laboratories">Sandia National Laboratories</a> and the <a href="/wiki/National_Renewable_Energy_Laboratory" title="National Renewable Energy Laboratory">National Renewable Energy Laboratory</a> (NREL), have heavily funded solar research programs. The NREL solar program has a budget of around $75 million<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> and develops research projects in the areas of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photovoltaic" title="Photovoltaic">photovoltaic</a> (PV) technology, solar thermal energy, and solar radiation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> The budget for Sandia's solar division is unknown, however it accounts for a significant percentage of the laboratory's $2.4 billion budget.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup>
</p><p>Several academic programs have focused on solar research in recent years. The Solar Energy Research Center (SERC) at <a href="/wiki/University_of_North_Carolina" title="University of North Carolina">University of North Carolina</a> (UNC) has the sole purpose of developing a cost-effective solar technology. In 2008, researchers at <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Institute_of_Technology" title="Massachusetts Institute of Technology">Massachusetts Institute of Technology</a> (MIT) developed a method to store solar energy by using it to produce hydrogen fuel from water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> Such research is targeted at addressing the obstacle that solar development faces of storing energy for use during nighttime hours when the sun is not shining. The Zhangebei National Wind and Solar Energy Storage and Transmission Demonstration Project northwest of Beijing, uses batteries to store 71 MWh, integrating wind and solar energy on the grid with frequency and voltage regulation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup>
</p><p>In February 2012, North Carolina-based Semprius Inc., a solar development company backed by German corporation <a href="/wiki/Siemens" title="Siemens">Siemens</a>, announced that they had developed the world's most efficient solar panel. The company claims that the prototype converts 33.9% of the sunlight that hits it to electricity, more than double the previous high-end conversion rate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wind">Wind</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Wind">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Wind_power" title="Wind power">Wind power</a></div>
<p>Wind energy research dates back several decades to the 1970s when <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> developed an analytical model to predict wind turbine power generation during high winds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> Today, both <a href="/wiki/Sandia_National_Laboratories" title="Sandia National Laboratories">Sandia National Laboratories</a> and <a href="/wiki/National_Renewable_Energy_Laboratory" title="National Renewable Energy Laboratory">National Renewable Energy Laboratory</a> have programs dedicated to wind research. Sandia's laboratory focuses on the advancement of materials, aerodynamics, and sensors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> The NREL wind projects are centered on improving wind plant power production, reducing their capital costs, and making wind energy more cost effective overall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Field Laboratory for Optimized Wind Energy (FLOWE) at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caltech" title="Caltech">Caltech</a> was established to research alternative approaches to wind energy farming technology practices that have the potential to reduce the cost, size, and environmental impact of wind energy production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup>
</p><p>Renewable energies such as wind, solar, biomass and geothermal combined, supplied 1.3% of global final energy consumption in 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biomass">Biomass</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Biomass">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Biomass" title="Biomass">Biomass</a> and <a href="/wiki/Biogas" title="Biogas">Biogas</a></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:229px;max-width:229px"><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:227px;max-width:227px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Faz_S_Sofia_canavial_090607_REFON.JPG"><img alt="" data-file-height="2176" data-file-width="3264" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Faz_S_Sofia_canavial_090607_REFON.JPG/225px-Faz_S_Sofia_canavial_090607_REFON.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Faz_S_Sofia_canavial_090607_REFON.JPG/338px-Faz_S_Sofia_canavial_090607_REFON.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Faz_S_Sofia_canavial_090607_REFON.JPG/450px-Faz_S_Sofia_canavial_090607_REFON.JPG 2x" width="225"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Sustainable_biofuel#Sugarcane_in_Brazil" title="Sustainable biofuel">Sugarcane plantation</a> to produce <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_fuel" title="Ethanol fuel">ethanol</a> in Brazil</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:227px;max-width:227px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Metz_biomass_power_station.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="519" data-file-width="531" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Metz_biomass_power_station.jpg/225px-Metz_biomass_power_station.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Metz_biomass_power_station.jpg/338px-Metz_biomass_power_station.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Metz_biomass_power_station.jpg/450px-Metz_biomass_power_station.jpg 2x" width="225"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">A <a href="/wiki/Cogeneration" title="Cogeneration">CHP power station</a> using wood to supplies 30,000 households in France</div></div></div></div>
<p>Biomass can be regarded as "<a href="/wiki/Biomaterial" title="Biomaterial">biological material</a>"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> derived from living, or recently living organisms. It most often refers to plants or plant-derived materials which are specifically called <a href="/wiki/Lignocellulosic_biomass" title="Lignocellulosic biomass">lignocellulosic biomass</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Biomass_Energy_Center_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-Biomass_Energy_Center-106">[106]</a></sup> As an energy source, biomass can either be used directly via combustion to produce heat, or indirectly after converting it to various forms of <a href="/wiki/Biofuel" title="Biofuel">biofuel</a>. Conversion of biomass to biofuel can be achieved by different methods which are broadly classified into: <i>thermal</i>, <i>chemical</i>, and <i>biochemical</i> methods. Wood remains the largest biomass energy source today;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-online.wsj.com_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-online.wsj.com-107">[107]</a></sup> examples include forest residues (such as dead trees, branches and <a href="/wiki/Tree_stump" title="Tree stump">tree stumps</a>), yard clippings, wood chips and even <a href="/wiki/Municipal_solid_waste" title="Municipal solid waste">municipal solid waste</a>. In the second sense, biomass includes plant or animal matter that can be converted into fibers or other industrial <a href="/wiki/Chemical_industry" title="Chemical industry">chemicals</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Biofuel" title="Biofuel">biofuels</a>. Industrial biomass can be grown from numerous types of plants, including <a href="/wiki/Miscanthus" title="Miscanthus">miscanthus</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Switchgrass" title="Switchgrass">switchgrass</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hemp" title="Hemp">hemp</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">corn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Populus" title="Populus">poplar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Willow" title="Willow">willow</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sorghum" title="Sorghum">sorghum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sugarcane" title="Sugarcane">sugarcane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bamboo" title="Bamboo">bamboo</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> and a variety of tree species, ranging from <a href="/wiki/Eucalyptus" title="Eucalyptus">eucalyptus</a> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oil_palm" title="Oil palm">oil palm</a> (<a href="/wiki/Palm_oil" title="Palm oil">palm oil</a>).
</p><p>Biomass, biogas and biofuels are burned to produce heat/power and in doing so harm the environment. Pollutants such as sulphurous oxides (SO<sub>x</sub>), nitrous oxides (NO<sub>x</sub>), and particulate matter (PM) are produced from this combustion. The World Health Organisation estimates that 7 million premature deaths are caused each year by air pollution,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-who.int_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-who.int-109">[109]</a></sup> and biomass combustion is a major contributor of it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-who.int_109-1"><a href="#cite_note-who.int-109">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> The use of biomas is carbon neutral over time, but is otherwise similar to burning fossil fuels.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethanol_biofuels">Ethanol biofuels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Ethanol biofuels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ethanol_biofuels" title="Ethanol biofuels">Ethanol biofuels</a></div>
<p>As the primary source of biofuels in North America, many organizations are conducting research in the area of <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">ethanol</a> production. On the Federal level, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USDA" title="USDA">USDA</a> conducts a large amount of research regarding ethanol production in the United States. Much of this research is targeted toward the effect of ethanol production on domestic food markets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/National_Renewable_Energy_Laboratory" title="National Renewable Energy Laboratory">National Renewable Energy Laboratory</a> has conducted various ethanol research projects, mainly in the area of <a href="/wiki/Cellulosic_ethanol" title="Cellulosic ethanol">cellulosic ethanol</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cellulosic_ethanol" title="Cellulosic ethanol">Cellulosic ethanol</a> has many benefits over traditional corn based-ethanol. It does not take away or directly conflict with the food supply because it is produced from wood, grasses, or non-edible parts of plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> Moreover, some studies have shown cellulosic ethanol to be more cost effective and economically sustainable than corn-based ethanol.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sandia_National_Laboratories" title="Sandia National Laboratories">Sandia National Laboratories</a> conducts in-house cellulosic ethanol research<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> and is also a member of the <a href="/wiki/Joint_BioEnergy_Institute" title="Joint BioEnergy Institute">Joint BioEnergy Institute</a> (JBEI), a research institute founded by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Energy" title="United States Department of Energy">United States Department of Energy</a> with the goal of developing cellulosic biofuels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_biofuels">Other biofuels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Other biofuels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Biofuel" title="Biofuel">Biofuel</a></div>
<p>From 1978 to 1996, the <a href="/wiki/National_Renewable_Energy_Laboratory" title="National Renewable Energy Laboratory">National Renewable Energy Laboratory</a> experimented with using algae as a biofuels source in the "<a href="/wiki/Aquatic_Species_Program" title="Aquatic Species Program">Aquatic Species Program</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> A self-published article by Michael Briggs, at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_New_Hampshire" title="University of New Hampshire">University of New Hampshire</a> Biofuels Group, offers estimates for the realistic replacement of all motor vehicle fuel with biofuels by utilizing algae that have a natural oil content greater than 50%, which Briggs suggests can be grown on algae ponds at wastewater treatment plants.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> This oil-rich algae can then be extracted from the system and processed into biofuels, with the dried remainder further reprocessed to create ethanol.
</p><p>The production of algae to harvest oil for biofuels has not yet been undertaken on a commercial scale, but feasibility studies have been conducted to arrive at the above yield estimate. In addition to its projected high yield, algaculture— unlike food crop-based biofuels — does not entail a decrease in food production, since it requires neither farmland nor fresh water. Many companies are pursuing algae bio-reactors for various purposes, including scaling up biofuels production to commercial levels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup>
</p><p>Several groups in various sectors are conducting research on Jatropha curcas, a poisonous shrub-like tree that produces seeds considered by many to be a viable source of biofuels feedstock oil.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> Much of this research focuses on improving the overall per acre oil yield of Jatropha through advancements in genetics, soil science, and horticultural practices. <a href="/wiki/SG_Biofuels" title="SG Biofuels">SG Biofuels</a>, a San Diego-based Jatropha developer, has used molecular breeding and biotechnology to produce elite hybrid seeds of Jatropha that show significant yield improvements over first generation varieties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> The Center for Sustainable Energy Farming (CfSEF) is a Los Angeles-based non-profit research organization dedicated to Jatropha research in the areas of plant science, agronomy, and horticulture. Successful exploration of these disciplines is projected to increase Jatropha farm production yields by 200-300% in the next ten years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Geothermal">Geothermal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Geothermal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geothermal_electricity" title="Geothermal electricity">Geothermal electricity</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Geothermal_energy" title="Geothermal energy">Geothermal energy</a> is produced by tapping into the heat within the earths crust. It is considered sustainable because that thermal energy is constantly replenished.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> However, the science of geothermal energy generation is still young and developing economic viability. Several entities, such as the <a href="/wiki/National_Renewable_Energy_Laboratory" title="National Renewable Energy Laboratory">National Renewable Energy Laboratory</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Sandia_National_Laboratories" title="Sandia National Laboratories">Sandia National Laboratories</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> are conducting research toward the goal of establishing a proven science around geothermal energy. The International Centre for Geothermal Research (IGC), a German geosciences research organization, is largely focused on geothermal energy development research.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hydrogen">Hydrogen</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Hydrogen">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_fuel" title="Hydrogen fuel">Hydrogen fuel</a></div>
<p>Over $1 billion has been spent on the research and development of hydrogen fuel in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> Both the <a href="/wiki/National_Renewable_Energy_Laboratory" title="National Renewable Energy Laboratory">National Renewable Energy Laboratory</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Sandia_National_Laboratories" title="Sandia National Laboratories">Sandia National Laboratories</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> have departments dedicated to hydrogen research. Much of this work centers on hydrogen storage and fuel cell technologies
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Disadvantages">Disadvantages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Disadvantages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy_debate" title="Renewable energy debate">Renewable energy debate</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Disadvantages_of_hydroelectricity" title="Disadvantages of hydroelectricity">Disadvantages of hydroelectricity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tidal_power#Tidal_power_issues" title="Tidal power">Tidal power § Tidal power issues</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Issues_relating_to_biofuels" title="Issues relating to biofuels">Issues relating to biofuels</a></div>
<p>The generation of alternative energy on the scale needed to replace fossil energy, in an effort to reverse global climate change, is likely to have significant negative environmental impacts. For example, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biomass_energy" title="Biomass energy">biomass energy</a> generation would have to increase 7-fold to supply current primary energy demand, and up to 40-fold by 2100 given economic and energy growth projections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> Humans already appropriate 30 to 40% of all photosynthetically fixed carbon worldwide, indicating that expansion of additional biomass harvesting is likely to stress ecosystems, in some cases precipitating collapse and extinction of animal species that have been deprived of vital food sources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> The total amount of energy capture by vegetation in the United States each year is around 58 quads (61.5 EJ), about half of which is already harvested as agricultural crops and forest products. The remaining biomass is needed to maintain ecosystem functions and diversity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> Since annual energy use in the United States is ca. 100 quads, biomass energy could supply only a very small fraction. To supply the current worldwide energy demand solely with biomass would require more than 10% of the Earth's land surface, which is comparable to the area use for all of world agriculture (i.e., ca. 1500 million hectares), indicating that further expansion of biomass energy generation will be difficult without precipitating an ethical conflict, given current world hunger statistics, over growing plants for biofuel versus food.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup>
</p><p>Given environmental concerns (e.g., fish migration, destruction of sensitive aquatic ecosystems, etc.) about building new dams to capture hydroelectric energy, further expansion of conventional <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydropower_in_the_United_States" title="Hydropower in the United States">hydropower in the United States</a> is unlikely. Windpower, if deployed on the large scale necessary to substitute fossil energy, is likely to face public resistance. If 100% of U.S. energy demand were to be supplied by wind power, about 80 million hectares (i.e., more than 40% of all available farmland in the United States) would have to be covered with wind turbines (50m hub height and 250 to 500 m apart).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> It is therefore not surprising that the major <a href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_of_wind_power" title="Environmental impact of wind power">environmental impact of wind power</a> is related to land use and less to wildlife (birds, bats, etc.) mortality. Unless only a relatively small fraction of electricity is generated by wind turbines in remote locations, it is unlikely that the public will tolerate large windfarms given concerns about blade noise and aesthetics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup>
</p><p>Biofuels are different from fossil fuels in regard to net greenhouse gases but are similar to fossil fuels in that biofuels contribute to <a href="/wiki/Air_pollution" title="Air pollution">air pollution</a>. Burning produces airborne carbon <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Particulate" title="Particulate">particulates</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nitrous_oxide" title="Nitrous oxide">nitrous oxides</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Disadvantages_2">Disadvantages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Disadvantages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Original_research" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><img alt="" data-file-height="40" data-file-width="40" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/40px-Ambox_important.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/60px-Ambox_important.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/80px-Ambox_important.svg.png 2x" width="40"/></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This article  <b>possibly contains <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research" title="Wikipedia:No original research">original research</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Alternative_energy&amp;action=edit">improve it</a> by <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verifying</a> the claims made and adding <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources#Inline_citations" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">inline citations</a>. Statements consisting only of original research should be removed.</span> <small><i>(March 2017)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Renewable alternative forms of energy have faced opposition from multiple groups, including conservatives and liberals. Around twelve states have passed proposals written to inhibit the alternative energy movement. Kansas lawmakers struck down a bill to phase out renewable energy mandates but face the possibility of the bill reappearing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-A_Pushback_on_Green_Power_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-A_Pushback_on_Green_Power-141">[141]</a></sup>
</p><p>The opposition cites the potentially high cost of branching out to these alternatives in order to support the continuation and reliance on fossil fuels. Ohio's mandate to phase in alternative energy faces opposition who believe higher electricity prices will result, while supporters fear the loss of economic development and jobs that alternative energy could bring.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-A_Pushback_on_Green_Power_141-1"><a href="#cite_note-A_Pushback_on_Green_Power-141">[141]</a></sup>
</p><p>With nuclear meltdowns in Chernobyl and Fukushima, nuclear power presents a constant danger and is more unlikely to be a popular alternative source.  The costs of maintaining nuclear facilities, the potential risk of meltdowns, and the cost of cleaning up meltdowns are cited as reasons behind the movement away from the use of nuclear energy.  In some countries nuclear power plants cannot compete with fossil fuels currently due to the latter's lower price and availability. Nuclear power plants also face competition from the increasing renewable energy subsidies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_New_York_Times_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_New_York_Times-142">[142]</a></sup>
</p><p>Solar panels are an icon of the 'green power' movement, however the process of manufacturing the quartz based panels can be detrimental to the environment. Raw quartz (silica) is used to create solar cells must be mined using harsh chemicals that harm the surrounding environment, as well as those working in the mines. Silicosis is a form of lung disease that is caused by the inhalation of crystalline silica dust resulting in nodule lesions in the lungs. The silica must be cultivated into metallurgical-grade silicon, the process requiring a massive amount of energy as the quartz is placed into electric arc furnaces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> The metallurgical grade silica must be processed into polysilicon. This process also produces tetrachloride, a toxic substance that, if not disposed of correctly, can be harmful to the surrounding environment. Hydrochloric acid is formed when tetrachloride interacts with water, lowering water and soil pH. Incidents of tetrachloride spills are common in China, as the production of solar panels has shifted from Europe and the United States to Asian countries within the early 2000s. Because of such, the villagers of Gaolong are unable to leave their homes due to air and soil becoming toxic. This was due to Luoyang Zhongui High-Technology Co. repeatedly dumped tetrachloride in a nearby field for almost a year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-13"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-13">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book"><a href="/wiki/Alexander_Graham_Bell" title="Alexander Graham Bell">Alexander Graham Bell</a> (1917). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=qBYSAAAAYAAJ" rel="nofollow"><i>Prizes for the Inventor: Some of the Problems Awaiting Solution</i></a>. <i>The National Geographic Magazine</i>. Alcohol makes a beautiful, clean and efficient fuel, and where not intended for human consumption can be manufactured very cheaply from corn stalks and in fact from almost any vegetable matter capable of fermentation.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Prizes+for+the+Inventor%3A+Some+of+the+Problems+Awaiting+Solution&amp;rft.pages=133&amp;rft.pub=National+Geographic+Society&amp;rft.date=1917&amp;rft.au=Alexander+Graham+Bell&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DqBYSAAAAYAAJ&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAlternative+energy"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-RFA1E-14"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-RFA1E_14-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20080408091334/http://www.ethanolrfa.org/industry/statistics/#E" rel="nofollow">"Industry Statistics: Annual World Ethanol Production by Country"</a>. Renewable Fuels Association. Archived from <a class="external text" href="http://www.ethanolrfa.org/industry/statistics/#E" rel="nofollow">the original</a> on 2008-04-08<span class="reference-accessdate">. <ul><li>Armstrong, Robert C., Catherine Wolfram, Robert Gross, Nathan S. Lewis, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/M.V._Ramana" title="M.V. Ramana">M.V. <li><a class="external text" href="http://spectrum.ieee.org/energy/renewables/a-skeptic-looks-at-alternative-energy/0" rel="nofollow">"A Skeptic Looks at Alternative Energy,"</a> by Vaclav Smil, <a href="/wiki/IEEE_Spectrum" title="IEEE Spectrum">IEEE Spectrum</a>, July 2012</li>
